```
Well, I personally know of at least two COBOL IS that if they started
writting code today (and there is no plan to even consider doing that)
it would take a decade or more to convert it all from COBOL to any other
language.  I also know of companies with millions of lines of COBOL
that is also not planned for conversion and they continue to write new
code (and make modifications to old code) in COBOL.  And, it doesn't help
that these particular niches are very conservative and resistant to any
kind of change. (banking, insurance, etc.)

While I expect it is unlikely that I will get my wish to go back to
full-time COBOL programming again I am totally certain that COBOL will
still be around in some of the most important places int he IT world 
long after I have shrugged off this mortal coil.

bill
```

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article
> <24688fa7-012c-47a9-9cb1-dff100f390d3@cd4g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>,
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I know you would expect me to reply to this, Bill, so I shall... :-)

> Well, I personally know of at least two COBOL IS that if they started
> writting code today (and there is no plan to even consider doing that)
> it would take a decade or more to convert it all from COBOL to any
> other language.

That is someone's estimate. Estimates are based on what people know. But 
very few people know everything.

How long it will take depnds on who is doing it and what tools they use.

I am currently working with a client who is converting their current flat 
file base to Relational Database. (It is a step towards migration off COBOL)

Before they came across the PRIMA Toolset, they estimated this job would 
take 2.5 man years. It was started last Monday and is now complete. (say 5.5 
days). They are now cleaning up and preparing to convert their existing 
legacy COBOL code base so it can access the new RDB instead of the old 
files. Original (multi-man-year) estimates for doing this have been 
discarded.

I will go there myself to provide support and walkthroughs on the Toolset 
(although they used it without problem for Data Conversion after watching 
the videos at http://primacomputing.co.nz/COBOL21/demosandtutorials.aspx ), 
but I don't expect to be away for more than a week.
(Unless I take a holiday afterwards; it is a nice part of Australia...)

Code Conversion of the legacy base is a bit more complex and I don't have 
videos done for it yet, so it is best to just pop over there. It is still a 
lot faster with tools than if you do it manually. (And the tools don't make 
careless errors from fatigue or lack of concentration.)

There are some excellent tools available (not just mine :-)) for people who 
want to convert COBOL to something else. Veryant are offering a good product 
and they are not the only ones. You can convert COBOL automatically to Java 
or C# and it DOESN"T take years. Personally, I don't favour doing that 
because it maintains the old paradigm in a new language, but there are 
certainly occasions when it would be a very viable option. and a step 
towards getting legacy encapsulated as objects.


 I also know of companies with millions of lines of
> COBOL
> that is also not planned for conversion and they continue to write new
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> kind of change. (banking, insurance, etc.)

As I specialized in Banking and Insurance for a number of years (household 
name institutions around the world) I am very familiar with the old chestnut 
that these people are still totally dependent on COBOL. COBOL IS important 
to many of them (although it is NOT, to just as many), but I don't know of 
ANY who are not currently planning to reduce or remove their dependence on 
COBOL going into the future.

So we have a different view on this. Even people (managers) who are "very 
conservative" (and I don't disagree there are many of these in the 
industries we are discussing), retire or move on. Sometimes, they get 
retired early BECAUSE they are "very conservative" in terms of technology, 
if not in terms of their business functionality.
>
> While I expect it is unlikely that I will get my wish to go back to
> full-time COBOL programming again I am totally certain that COBOL will
> still be around in some of the most important places int he IT world
> long after I have shrugged off this mortal coil.

I hope you'll be with us for a considerable time yet, Bill :-)

Pete.
```

```
On 9 Sep., 15:46, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
> In article <24688fa7-012c-47a9-9cb1-dff100f39...@cd4g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>,
>         HansJ <hans.i...@googlemail.com> writes:
…[58 more quoted lines elided]…
> Scranton, Pennsylvania   |         #include <std.disclaimer.h>  

Bill,

I've been very sceptical about automated conversion from COBOL to Java
for quite some time. I've however seen some usefull samples for
complex subroutines used in an insurance environment where they tried
to use the same source base. I don't know how this turned out as it is
quite a while ago now.

I'm however also aware of a few very critical sites, one is banking,
where an automatd COBOL to Java conversion worked out really well.
Another is a north american police department of a fairly large city.

There have benn good reasons to go this path that might not apply
elsewhere, but in those cases it did the job in a consistent way and
really fast. Though the converted code is still "procedural", all the
new code to be developed from now on is running in the same open
environment and does introduce proper object oriented methods.

The alternative would have been to support the legacy system on a
proprietary platform for the time a new replacement system is
implemented. This was definitely less attractive.

Regards Hans
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol jokes

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-09-10T14:32:58+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9d00isFkrsU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<8f0964ad-b00b-425f-b759-24db3d54b360@s20g2000yql.googlegroups.com> <9c63k4Ft9uU1@mid.individual.net> <79ns57949sm2ot0b6p63e8gvo67fiic8ej@4ax.com> <j42ti7$8l9$5@reader1.panix.com> <f3256255-9f8b-46b7-9832-0d84ff39de83@w28g2000yqw.googlegroups.com> <9cug6cFhirU1@mid.individual.net> <24688fa7-012c-47a9-9cb1-dff100f390d3@cd4g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
HansJ wrote:
> On 9 Sep., 14:47, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> I'll buy you a good meal.

I'm not sure whether that was a lucky guess or whether you have inferred my 
delight in dining, Hans. :-)

If there's good food going, I'm there... :-)

Food is something very dear to my heart (I love cooking it, eating it and 
sharing it with others). But I wasn't aware that I had consciously indicated 
that here.  This particular indulgence means I have to watch my weight and I 
learned many years ago that eating delicious food doesn't mean you have to 
become obese. I am 1.83 metres (around 6 feet) tall and never weigh over 100 
KG (220 pounds).  As an ex-Rugby player I have a large enough frame to carry 
this and I'm not afraid to get into a swimsuit. It is achieved by eating 
sensibly and going for healthy foods.  I don't get as much exercise as I 
should (although now the Summer is coming here I hope to address that) but 
it is fitness levels that suffer rather than general health.

Anyway, I will gladly accept your offer, but let's be sure it is fair.

Sometime back in the '90s I stated that COBOL would no longer be the 
development language of choice by the year 2015.

This was at a time when Gartner were saying that astronomical numbers of 
lines of COBOL could never be converted or thrown away and  the implication 
was that COBOL was still a good career choice and it's future was not in 
doubt. (Gartner have since backtracked on those claims but there are vested 
COBOL interests who have not given the same publicity to the recant which 
they did to the original.)

(Many people, particularly in this forum, were pleased to hear it and lapped 
it up...)

I made my statement because I realised that the paradigm had changed, that 
the new one was "better" (in terms of where IT was going, which was to 
Networked solutions), and that COBOL was no longer cost effective. (This 
last is the REAL major factor for the decline of COBOL). For many years 
there was no choice; you HAD to maintain thousands  (millions?) of lines of 
COBOL. It was tricky and demanding. A one line change can have impacts in 
programs way downline and the effects can require considerable time, 
application, and skill to run down and fix. The fix causes more problems and 
the whole cycle repeats itself. Not only that, but the tools provided for 
working with COBOL were woefully inadequate, yet people just accepted them 
because they hadn't seen anything better.

Object orientation allowed the building of components that could encapsulate 
functionality and be re-used. They were visible only through an interface 
and they could have multiple interfaces. If the logic in a component was 
found to be flawed you could fix it, secure in the knowledge that NOTHING 
outside that component would be affected. In fact, components were usually 
small enough that you could rewrite the entire component in less time than 
it took to fix standard COBOL... It went even further as people realised 
that you could buy packaged functionality and plug it into your 
applications. If it didn't do what it said on the box the vendor would 
replace it. (In the 25 years I have been using component based development 
with a mix of third party components as well as my own, I only ever had one 
occasion to contact a component vendor. They rectified the problem within 24 
hours. It probably would have taken more than that to locate and fix it in 
COBOL source...) There was no longer any need to enshrine Source Code; 
FUNCTIONALITY was everything.

COBOL people just sniffed and said "We've been writing modular code for 
years...". The other implications of OO programming  (and they are legion) 
were never discovered by them. Meanwhile, outside COBOL a new IT world, 
based on the new paradigm was being constructed. A new generation of  more 
"computer literate" IT users was arriving in the workplace (with higher 
expectations than their predecessors who had simply had to accept whatever 
the COBOL guys gave them...) and a new generation of programmers was coming 
out of Acadaemia. They simply didn't need COBOL any more.

If you were to compare the role of COBOL in the IT world of the l970s 
through to perhaps the mid 1990s, with the role of COBOL in the IT world of 
today, I think I could claim that my statement is already vindicated. COBOL 
is no longer the development language of choice for just about anybody. (Can 
we agree that less than 2% of the world's development constitutes 
"nobody"?). COBOL is now around the 30th most popular development language 
(and falling), when it was in the top five for over 30 years.

On that basis I could already claim my meal with you :-).

However, when I said  "'Nother three years I reckon..." it was a 
light-hearted allusion to my previous 2015 date. (yes, it should have been 
"four years" but I was tired when I wrote it... :-))

Having said all of the above, there IS a place for COBOL legacy and it will 
be with us for a little while yet. COBOL is excellent at batch processing 
and there are still many systems around that require batch processing. 
Similarly, there are many mainframe systems using CICS for their transaction 
processing and it will be some time before all of that is replaced. (But, 
you can be certain it WILL be replaced. The question is WHEN not IF)

Modern systems avoid batch processing. The idea is that you design a data 
warehouse/repository/database that can model the real world and use 
distributed processors to update it in real time, and derive information 
from it. At any given moment, the data store reflects reality so there is no 
need to update it overnight. "period boundaries" (delimited by dates) are 
processed on the basis that every piece of information in the store has a 
timestamp derivable for it, if not explicity placed in a DB row.  Instead of 
just monthly intervals being reported, you can set start and end dates for 
ANY period you like and the information can be presented to you on a screen 
instantly or on a printed report (derived from the screen display, or built 
with tools like stimulsoft and Crystal (no specifc coding for reports) . 
High frequency or key reports may be built in real time line by line as the 
transactions which would generate them occur. This is made possible by 
parallel processing using distributed processors. It is a far cry from the 
centralized processor of the COBOL world.

Maybe you would like to define what you see as being fair for me to claim my 
meal (or not), Hans. :-)

Pete.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:

Alistair, control of mental imagery is one of the defining factors for 
sanity.

Your head is your problem. :-)

There are some beaches here where you don't actually need the swimsuit but 
there is still a lot of Victorian conservatism in our "culture".  Our town 
was recently embroiled in a raging debate over whether a procession of 
topless women on motorcycles (Boobs on bikes) should be allowed.

Some of the comments were really quite frightening to me. Things like: "It 
is the work of Satan and if this goes ahead we let him win..." "I want my 
children protected from this filth..." and so on. Fortunately, reason 
prevailed and the procession was a great success. Boys from the local 
Colleges cut school to go and see it and one Headmaster was patrolling the 
City rounding up his pupils. A good crowd turned out, local businesses were 
very happy, and a small group of protesters who put a chain across the road 
only succeeded in delaying the procession longer so everybody had a good 
photo opportunity. I didn't go myself, but only because I had something more 
interesting to do at the time, not because I objected in principle.

A City Councillor who saw a chance to win support form the God Squad tried 
to stop it on legal grounds (indecent exposure) and found, to his horror, 
(and my amusement), that there was already a Supreme Court ruling that in 
New Zealand female nudity of the upper body is NOT considered offensive by 
the majority of people, and is therefore NOT illegal.

I enjoyed living in Germany where they grow up with a much more healthy and 
less repressed attitude to their bodies. I used to go for a swim and a sauna 
every Sunday and became accustomed to having perfectly normal conversations 
with naked people (men and women) while myself being naked. At first I was a 
bit nonplussed, but after a couple of weeks I thought no more of it than 
they did. We all have bodies and we really should come to terms with that.

Pete.
```

```
It is rare, nowadays, but there will still be times when I see a 
motorcyclist riding without a helmet.  My first thought is always a 
moderately selfish 'I support your right to do that and I hope you die 
before you have children; Get Those Genes Out of My Pool!'

When I rode it was with a helmet and the standard leather jacket, heavy 
denim pants and a set of ankle-covering work boots.  When I had to lay it 
down (motorcyclist's slang for sliding the bike onto it's side in order to 
avert greater tragedy) I followed the mantra of 'Leather before skin, skin 
before hide, hide before bone, bone before life.'

(Despite all this I managed to fracture a few... bah, folks have heard 
this (or the same) from other geezers before.)
```

```
I found out in much later life that my Mother (who didn't seem to me to be 
in the least an adventurous type) had raced motorcycles on a large expanse 
of Northland called 90 mile beach, when she was 19.  She broke several ribs 
(as well as winning several trophies). I couldn't understand her fierce 
resistance to my getting a small 250cc Jawa when I was a teenager. (At that 
time I never knew about her motorcycle racing). A couple of excursions on 
crowed roads in the rain persuaded me to give it up. "Four wheels good; two 
wheels bad..." :-) and I managed to get an ancient (1936) Morris 8 which was 
much too slow to ever be dangerous, but served the double purpose of taking 
me where I wanted to go and getting me interested in cars and tinkering with 
them.

Since then, on occasion I have ridden pillion on the motorcycles of some 
very proficient and experienced riders and I agree it is a fantastic 
experience to be on a bike, but I still love cars better.

Now I'm wondering if you were ever a patched member of a Motorcycle club, 
Doc?

Pete.
```

```
>I couldn't understand her fierce

The Jawa?  A blast from the past, both barrels.  I recall hearing them 
described as being similar to Soviet-era wool suits; one good wearing and 
you could never get a crease in them again but the saggy, shapeless 
clothing wore like iron unto eternity.

>(At that 
>time I never knew about her motorcycle racing). A couple of excursions on 
>crowed roads in the rain persuaded me to give it up.

I was taught that riding in the rain is fine as long as you treat the road 
surface as if it was a sheet of glass covered with a thin layer of 
oil-soaked ball bearings.  Slow down and enjoy the moisture; keep in mind 
that you can only get so wet in rain but in snow you can freeze to death.

[snip]

>Now I'm wondering if you were ever a patched member of a Motorcycle club, 
>Doc?

I never wore colors, Mr Dashwood, but years after I gave up riding I spent 
time in a biker-bar (all indies) in order to learn some proper manners.

(Once, while visiting a friend, I saw another bar with a row of large 
motorcycles outside of it.  I walked in... it was a Saturday afternoon, 
about 2:pm, and folks were still waking up.  I said to the bartender 'My 
father told me that if you see a row of scoots outside a bar there's 
always an honest man inside...'

... and the bartender grinned...

'... and he's usually in the back, tied to a chair and beaten half to 
death.  Would you bring him out so I might meet him?'

The bartender dropped his head with a groan and said 'It's too early for 
this.  It's on me, what are you drinking?')

DD
```

```
... motorcyles ...

I always had a motorcyle since I was 18 years of age, the first years
because a car was to expensive, later for fun. Fortunately, I never
had any accident, though I "did lay it down" a few times. Using the
proper gear is essential and riding a bike without a helmet and
leather jacket etc. (for more than just a few meters) is showing a
lack of responisbility. I still have a decent 1000cc Kawasaki sitting
idle, but registered and insured in the garage for a couple of years
now. As time goes by other things get more attention.

Last summer I did sell a 67 Honda 250cc and another 550cc Honda from
75 that I had owned for decades to a friend who was about to restore
them.

Now a short thing that happened a couple of years back when I was
refueling on a gas station in Vienna Austria.

A motorcyclist was speeding up on the road in front of the gas station
and doing a wheelie for a few hundred meters, a fellow fueling up
right besides me looked at him and then to me saying, "more work to
come". He was a physician an his way to work in a hospital.

Desaster for one guy makes a living for the other one.

Hans
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re:OT Motocycles

_(thread depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-09-18T12:41:29-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<NISdnRDoLfyzguvTnZ2dnUVZ_uadnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<9dgq3dFf13U1@mid.individual.net> <b8a0558320d5cd64ab70b00f64bd5733@msgid.frell.theremailer.net> <a080196e-c49c-4610-96e5-5fa888b96e79@1g2000vbu.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair Maclean" <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:a080196e-c49c-4610-96e5-5fa888b96e79@1g2000vbu.googlegroups.com...
On Sep 17, 7:06 pm, Fritz Wuehler
<fr...@spamexpire-201109.rodent.frell.theremailer.net> wrote:
> > > alive (face not covered). No crash helmet, gloves or leathers in
> > > evidence on the biker.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Those of us who ride know the difference.

>I also ride a motorbike (Kawasaki ZZR 600) and have ridden motorbikes
>since I was 17.

>I would still argue that the man was a motorcyclist (abbreviated to
>biker) but also very much an IDIOT; that being the most accurate
>description of riding helemetless, gloveless and jacketless.

In my early twenties my first motorcycle was a Yamaha 175 cc.  This was a 
dirt bike and we rode off road along the power line routes.  Used to race a 
little and jump them through the  air.  I still have a bump on my right heel 
from the first day of riding.  I kept stalling it, and kickstarting it, and 
my heel kept hitting the rear peg for the passengers foot.  This was a two 
stroke engine and it was hard to get more than 20 miles on a single spark 
plug.  The oil kept fouling them.  I tried adjusting the float countless 
times but never could get any increases mileage from the plugs.  I have done 
the "flying W" over the handle bars and once my leg was trapped between a 
log and the exhaust pipe and no hair grows there(on my leg) to this day.  I 
remember a motorcycle movie "On Any Sunday" with Steve McQueen and others. 
Boy those guys could really ride.

My second and last motocycle was a Honda 250 cc.  It had an upswept pipe so 
it was considered an off road bike but I also used it on the highway.  This 
was a four stroke and I liked it much better.  The only time I went down on 
it I was going about 2 miles an hour over some curving railroad tracks. 
They were wet and the rear tire slide down the tracks.  I climbed up on top 
of the bike but did hurt my knee a little.  Once I was riding through the 
mountains on a dirt road and some green bug hit a bare spot on my leg.  It 
burned like hell for several hours.  Once I stopped and saw a bear off in 
the distance heading my way.  It was a little scary but when he heard the 
motor running he turned around and went the other way.  I enjoyed riding the 
dirt fire road in the mountains.

I did experience a few close calls  from cars on the highway.  Finally one 
day while returning home I stopped at a light at least 10 feet away from the 
car in front of me.  The guy behind me stopped but the woman behind him hit 
him and pushed his car into my bike. This knocked me into the car in front 
and I almost went down.  The curved bumper helped me to keep it up and the 
car behind me ended up partially on top of my rear wheel.  My mom who had 
previously had a heart attack passed by the accident on her way home and saw 
my Michigan windbreaker hanging from the handlebars.  Thank God she did not 
have another heart attack.  All of this made me finally decide to give up 
motorcycle riding on the street.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol jokes

_(thread depth: 19)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2011-09-18T11:27:15-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<r5ac77p1gti85o0kpnnu0vfsej7krl61a9@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9dgq3dFf13U1@mid.individual.net> <b8a0558320d5cd64ab70b00f64bd5733@msgid.frell.theremailer.net> <a080196e-c49c-4610-96e5-5fa888b96e79@1g2000vbu.googlegroups.com>`

```
I was attending Drake University and riding my motorcycle to its Chess
club one evening (the same day as I had my draft physical - which I
shouldn't have had as I was in AFROTC).    A car pulled out of a bar
and knocked me and my chess pieces across 4 lanes of road.   I messed
up my Father's WWII leather jacket, I messed up my leather boots &
gloves.   My helmet was still good.    The driver stopped (saving him
the felony hit and run), then left (saving him the felony drunk
driving), and was later charged with leaving the scene of an accident.

I knew we should get the license of someone doing that, but that isn't
why I had his license plate embedded in my leg.

I ended up with a year of college paid for (I switched to Iowa State
the next year as I was a resident then).    That was the year with my
worst GPA, as I had plenty of time to study.   When I worked full
time, I needed to make time to study, so I didn't put it off.
```

<a id="t-9d93275a6d"></a>
## Thread: Netcobol for windows web services — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web)_

### Netcobol for windows web services

- **From:** Emerson <emersonlopes@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-04T09:52:57-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50b925a9-58c0-462b-b9a9-ade04f4bd6b5@l28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello,

I would like to share a little example of how to create a web services
using Netcobol. It can be consumed by any language, not only Cobol,
but I'm also providing a Powercobol sample of how to call it.

Regards, Emerson


*-----------------------------------------------------------------------
*
*   All Rights Public, Copyleft(C) Emerson
Lopes                        *
*
*
* - Web Service usando Fujitsu NetCobol for
Windows                     *
*-----------------------------------------------------------------------
*
 IDENTIFICATION   DIVISION.
 PROGRAM-ID       webServices.
 ENVIRONMENT      DIVISION.
 CONFIGURATION    SECTION.
 repository.
     class ClassCOM as "*COM"
     class ClassCOMException as "*COM-EXCEPTION".

 DATA             DIVISION.

 WORKING-STORAGE  SECTION.

 01  errorSource                    pic x(1024).
 01  errorMessage                   pic x(1024).

 01  cgiParameters.
     05  resultado                  pic x(512).  *> Defina o tamanho
conforme o necessário
     05  paramName                  pic x(128).
     05  paramValue                 pic x(1024). *> Se necessário,
aumente este limite
     05  keyName                    pic x(128).
     05  webMethod                  pic x(32) value
"NO_WEB_METHOD_PASSED".
     05  indice                     pic s9(9) comp-5 value 0.

 01  nodeName                       pic x(128).
 01  soapPrefix                     pic x(128).
 01  xmlDOMClass                    pic x(30) value
"Microsoft.XMLDOM".
 01  XmlDOM                         object reference ClassCOM.
 01  xmlNodes                       object reference ClassCOM.
 01  xmlFirstChild                  object reference ClassCOM.
 01  XmlNode                        object reference ClassCOM.
 01  documentElement                object reference ClassCOM.

 COPY COBW3.

*
 PROCEDURE        DIVISION.
 declaratives.
 ErrorEvent section.
      use after exception ClassCOMException.

         invoke exception-object "GET-SOURCE"      returning
errorSource
         invoke exception-object "GET-DESCRIPTION" returning
errorMessage

         string errorSource  delimited by size
                " "          delimited by size
                errorMessage delimited by size
           into resultado

         perform setResponseHello
         perform returnResponse
         perform freeResources

         exit program.

 end declaratives.

 main.
     perform initializeCGI
     perform getSOAP
     perform getWebMethod

     evaluate webMethod
              when "helloCobol"
                   perform buildResponseHello
                   perform setResponseHello

           *> when "outro webmethod"
                   *> ...

              when "NO_WEB_METHOD_PASSED"
                   move "SOAP incorreto!!!"   to  resultado
                   perform setResponseHello
     end-evaluate

     perform returnResponse
     perform freeResources

     exit program.


 initializeCGI.
     move    LOW-VALUE                to  COBW3

     call    "COBW3_INIT" using COBW3.

     move    "text/xml; charset=UTF-8"   to  COBW3-CONTENT-TYPE  *>
definindo o content-type de retorno do web service

     call    "COBW3_PUT_HEAD" using COBW3

     set     COBW3-PHYSICALPATH              to  true  *> definindo
caminho físico até o arquivo XML, baseado na localização do programa
CGI

     call    "COBW3_GET_REQUEST_INFO" using COBW3

     move    COBW3-REQUEST-INFO              to  COBW3-HTML-FILENAME.

     move    "\webService.xml"               to  COBW3-HTML-
FILENAME(COBW3-REQUEST-INFO-LENGTH + 1:). *> definindo template XML
SOAP a ser usado

 getSOAP.
     set     COBW3-QUERYSTRING               to  TRUE *> solicitando
web parameter

     call    "COBW3_GET_REQUEST_INFO" using COBW3

     if      COBW3-REQUEST-INFO-LENGTH = zero
             move  "request vazio"    to  resultado
             perform setResponseHello
             perform returnResponse
             perform freeResources
             exit program
     end-if

     invoke  ClassCOM "CREATE-OBJECT"    using xmlDOMClass returning
xmlDOM *> Criando instância do PARSER XML

     invoke  xmlDOM "loadXML"            using COBW3-REQUEST-INFO. *>
Carregando XML recebido com resultado no PARSER

 getWebMethod.
     *> Definindo qual webMethod foi chamado
     invoke  xmlDOM "GET-documentElement" returning xmlNode   *>
Recuperando XML como um objeto DOM

     if      xmlNode not = NULL
             invoke  xmlNode       "GET-firstChild" returning
xmlFirstChild    *> Recuperando elemento BODY
             invoke  xmlFirstChild "GET-firstChild" returning
xmlFirstChild    *> Recuperando node com nome do web method

             invoke  xmlFirstChild "GET-nodeName" returning
nodeName

             unstring nodeName delimited by ":" into soapPrefix
webMethod  *> Recuperando o nome do método

             if      webMethod = spaces     *> se o SOAP não tiver
prefixo...
                     move   nodeName   to  webMethod
             end-if
     end-if.

  buildResponseHello.
     *> Recuperando parâmetro de entrada
     invoke  xmlDOM "GET-documentElement" returning xmlNode   *>
Recuperando XML como um objeto DOM

             invoke  xmlNode       "GET-firstChild" returning
xmlFirstChild    *> Recuperando elemento BODY
             invoke  xmlFirstChild "GET-firstChild" returning
xmlFirstChild    *> Recuperando node com nome do web method

     move    "//name"                    to  paramName

     invoke  xmlDOM "selectSingleNode" using paramName returning
xmlNode   *> Recuperando node com parâmetro

     invoke  xmlNode "GET-TEXT" returning
paramValue.                     *> Recuperando valor do parâmetro

     string  "Hello, "                        delimited by size
             paramValue                       delimited by space
             ", welcome to WebServices World" delimited by size
        into resultado.

  setResponseHello.
     *> Setando parâmetro de retorno
     move    "Mensagem"                      to  COBW3-CNV-NAME

     set    COBW3-CNV-MODE-ADDREP            to  TRUE.

     move   0                                to  COBW3-CNV-VALUE-
LENGTH.
     move   function LENG("Mensagem")        to  COBW3-CNV-NAME-
LENGTH.
     move    resultado                       to  COBW3-CNV-VALUE

     call   "COBW3_SET_CNV_XX" using COBW3.

   returnResponse.
     *> enviando XML para o client do web services
     call   "COBW3_PUT_HTML" using COBW3.

  freeResources.
     call    "COBW3_FREE" using COBW3.

This is XML SOAP returned by the Cobol web services
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="utf-8"?>
     <soap:Envelope xmlns:soap="http://schemas.xmlsoap.org/soap/
envelope/" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance"
xmlns:xsd="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema">
	     <soap:Body>
            <Parametros xmlns="http://tempuri.org/">
                <Name>//COBOL//Mensagem//COBOL//</Name>
            </Parametros>
         </soap:Body>
     </soap:Envelope>


This is a simple Powercobol sample that show how to consume the web
services above

 ENVIRONMENT     DIVISION.
 DATA            DIVISION.
 WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
 01  URI         pic x(256) value "http://www.oocobol.net/
NetCobolWebServices.wsdl".
 01  soapClient  pic x(128) value "MSSOAP.SoapClient30".

 01  webService  usage object reference COM.
 01  webMethod   pic x(256) value "helloCobol".

 01  nome     pic x(256) value "Emerson".

 01  outRetorno  pic x(8192).

 PROCEDURE       DIVISION.

     invoke COM "CREATE-OBJECT" using soapClient returning webService

     invoke webService "mssoapinit" using URI

     move   "Text" of CmText1     to  nome

     invoke webService webMethod using nome returning outRetorno

     INVOKE MainForm "DisplayMessage" USING outRetorno

If you wish to consume the Cobol web services using another language,
here is the WSDL file (yes, it is online!!!)

http://www.oocobol.net/NetCobolWebServices.wsdl


I'll soon publish an article explaining it in details.

Regards, Emerson

www.100coolthings.net

PS: I've created a precompiler for Netcobol that make life easier. It
will be available soon for everybody.
```

```
impressed by the recitation of his own history... taught here, lectured 
there, published this-and-that... and he began to get a bit proud...

... and was gently reminded of his place by hearing the much shorter 
introduction of another man: 'Ladies and gentlemen, the President of the 
United States'.

(for non-citizens: the Honorific of Office is 'Mr President')
```

#### ↳ Re: Replacing and combine

- **From:** Emerson <emersonlopes@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-10T17:17:34-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2affb4d4-4b30-4d1f-a5bc-739bcd7a5e8c@h7g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9e6a2ace-5941-4e50-b970-a6c205b727b5@t29g2000vby.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 19 ago, 09:21, jmoore <jmoore...@gmail.com> wrote:
> I need to make fields with punctuation removed and combined. ie:
> FM-25 to FM25. What is the best way?

Hi,

I'm not sure if this would be the best way, but certainly it works for
me. The following are two method that I've created for my Cobol
precompiler class. You may find it useful (I do!).

  usage:

   to replace a substring with nothing
   invoke yourClassInstance "ReplaceText" using textTobeReplaced
"whateverYouWant"  singlespace returning texttobereplaced

   to replace a string with something else
   invoke yourClassInstance "ReplaceText" using "Cobol is dead"
"dead" "very alive!"  returning finalText


   it could be improved by using dynamic memory allocation etc but a
1024 chars limit is all that I need.




 method-id. ReplaceText.
 data division.
 working-storage section.
 01  sourceString         pic x(1024).
 01  tobeReplaced         pic x(1024).
 01  toReplaceWith        pic x(1024).
 01  newString            pic x(1024).
 01  temp                 pic x(1024).
 01  sourceIndexOf        pic 9(04).
 01  newCurrChar          pic 9(04).
 01  nextSubString        pic 9(04).

 linkage section.
 01  inString             pic x any length.
 01  inSubString1         pic x any length.
 01  inSubString2         pic x any length.
 01  outString            pic x any length.

 procedure division using inString inSubString1 inSubString2 returning
outString.

     move   spaces        to  newString
     move   inString      to  sourceString
     move   inSubString1  to  tobeReplaced
     move   inSubString2  to  toReplaceWith
     move   1             to  newCurrChar

     perform until function stored-char-length(sourceString) = 0
             *> looking for substring
             move  self::"IndexOf"(sourceString, tobeReplaced)   to
sourceIndexOf
             *> if does not exist any more, get out
             if    sourceIndexOf = zero
                   move   sourceString                           to
newString(newCurrChar:)
                   exit perform
             end-if

             *> check if substring exists after first char to move
whatever exist before to the new string
             if    sourceIndexOf > 1
                   move  sourceString(1:sourceIndexOf - 1)    to
newString(newCurrChar:)
                   compute newCurrChar = function stored-char-
length(newString) + 1
             end-if

             move   toReplaceWith                               to
newString(newCurrChar:)

             if     toReplaceWith > spaces
                    add   function stored-char-
length(toReplaceWith)    to  newCurrChar
             end-if

             move  sourceString(sourceIndexOf + function stored-char-
length(tobeReplaced):)   to  temp
             move  temp                                         to
sourceString
     end-perform

     move    newString            to  outString

 end method ReplaceText.

 method-id. IndexOf.
 data division.
 working-storage section.
 01  indice      pic 9(5).
 01  temp        pic x(1024).
 01  temp2       pic x(1024).

 linkage section.
 01  inString        pic x any length.
 01  inSubString     pic x any length.
 01  outIndexOf      pic 9(5).

 procedure division using inString inSubString returning outIndexOf.
     if    inString = spaces or low-values
           move  zero                    to  outIndexOf
           exit  method
     end-if

     move  1                             to  outIndexOf
     move  inString                      to  temp
     move  function upper-case(temp)     to  temp
     move  inSubstring                   to  temp2
     move  function upper-case(temp2)    to  temp2

     perform until outIndexOf > function stored-char-length(temp)
             if    temp(outIndexOf:function stored-char-length(temp2))
= temp2
                   exit method
             end-if
             add   1         to  outIndexOf
     end-perform

     move    zero            to  outIndexOf

 end method IndexOf.
```

```
In PL/I, there's PLIEBCDIC and PLIASCII,
These convert ASCII to EBCDIC, and EBCDIC to ASCII, respectively.
Have you checked to see whether your compiler offers something
similar?
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Using OpenCobol to convert a fixed size EBCDIC file to ASCII?

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-09T16:01:00-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<l0waq.192256$7C3.9618@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<7cd33e25-b4a7-4724-8da4-3dfa765ed793@ea4g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <6f588b9a-25ad-4bab-885c-04686659d2e5@a10g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`

```
The first question in converting files from ASCII to EBCDIC is whether you 
have both character and comutational data or not.  If your COBOL program 
only has "PIC X" type data, then the conversion is easy with or without 
COBOL.  If your file (as most do) has mixed PIC X and COMP, BINARY, 
PACED-DECIMAL (etc) fields, and especially if there are multiple types of 
records (with different lay-outs), then you need to do an "intellegent" 
conversion

Writing a COBOL program to do an "intellegent conversion" is very easy if 
you understand MOVE CORR and CODE-SET syntax and YES, you can easily do this 
in OpenCOBOL.

> Hi guys,
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> that I need to convert from EBCDIC to ASCII ASAP!!
```

<a id="t-6bc6764e66"></a>
## Thread: The value of stupidity. — 13 msgs

### The value of stupidity.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-09-10T21:34:36+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9d0p9dF7usU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Two things happened to me this week which made me stop and think.

1. Somebody I was helping solve a programming problem said to me: "Jeez, 
Pete, you have a lot more tenacity than me. I would've given up some time 
ago."  I assumed she was hinting we take a coffee break, but over coffee she 
expanded a bit. It turned out she felt she was just too stupid to solve some 
problems. I happen to know she is a very intelligent young woman and an 
excellent programmer (in COBOL and JavaScriot (if that counts... :-)) After 
coffee we went back to the problem and solved it. But I couldn't get over 
her self-doubt.

Then I remembered something from the past. I was a young programmer and had 
been tasked with the job of amending a COBOL compiler to do various things. 
(A major one was to patch it so it could read input from disk or tape, 
indstead of punched cards, so that will give you an idea of how long ago 
this was....) There was no documentation for it (it was written for 
Burroughs by a contractor who had since moved on and they weren't supporting 
it, but our business revolved around it.)

All I remember feeling at the time was a heap of doubt. I couldn't do this. 
WE didn't amend system software, people in Detroit, Michigan did that... It 
was totally unfair. I would get fired.  I remember feeling stupid and 
useless. (I am actually neither of those things, but this was a long time 
ago :-)) Fortunately, I had a Boss who was excellent and was also far from 
stupid. He motivated by example and wrote an entire Operating System for a 
machine he had never seen, in an Assembler language he didn't know, in the 
space of around three weeks, in his spare time. (And it ran in production 
for over three years without ever failing.)

I started brushing up my Assembler for the platform and disassembled the 
compiler. My Boss was supportive and encouraging and I really wanted to 
impress him. Two weeks later I had achieved all the things we were looking 
for and I had documented what each pass of the compiler did and things of 
special interest in it. I was a hero, completely unstoppable. :-)

Ever since then I have worked on the assumption that software written by 
humans can be fixed by humans, it just takes time and application. If 
somebody tells me: "That's not possible" I want an explanation as to WHY it 
isn't. Otherwise it falls in the category of : "YOU don't know how to do it, 
and you're saying it's impossible, just to cover your arse."  (An honest 
person would say:"I don't know how to do that, but I'll investigate the 
possibilities if you want me to." Such people have my respect and usually 
end up with my admiration as well.)

 You also have to believe that you are capable of learning anything. (You 
actually are, but life and circumstances, mixed with COIs and 
disappointments, persuade you you are only good at what you know. Not true. 
Given the right motivation you can learn anything.)

When  a certain COBOL vendor couldn't maintain their own product and left me 
hanging on a customer site, I took their product apart and have never needed 
their help since. (In fact nowadays I help others with it...)
It ISN'T stupid to not know something. It IS stupid to not WANT to know it, 
when it pertains to your livelihood.


2. I came across this article which raises a very pertinent point.  How many 
times in your IT career are you faced with the "unknown"? "Never been 
trained on that." "Don't know anything about this...".  Somebody comes to 
you and wants to port COBOL to a Bendix washing machine because their 
toaster just popped its clogs...  Nobody will touch it.

Be the one.

Apart from money and the satisfaction in helping people, there is the real 
joy of knowing you conquered something, that most people wouldn't even look 
at. I know from personal experience that delivering something which they 
said "Couldn't be done..." is one of the sweetest things in life.

In the Article, the author uses the example of a PhD thesis as an instance 
of delving into the unkown. But there are many times in IT when it happens 
too.

http://jcs.biologists.org/content/121/11/1771.full

Enjoy!

Pete.
```

```
an application or process I have had little or no knowledge of.  Heaps
of praise (but no money) have been laid on me when I come up with
solutions that are both efficient and feasible.  I approach each of
these challenges the same way I approach an issue with my PC.  You
have to be smarter than the --------- (whatever).  Take your time,
tear it apart, research it, throw things at it and see what sticks,
but most of all , don't give up or give in.
```

#### ↳ Re: The value of stupidity.

- **From:** Mattc <mpcsoft6@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-11T06:02:59-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<99fb7cc7-175f-49b0-b6e2-04aad0666af6@y21g2000yqk.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9d0p9dF7usU1@mid.individual.net>`

```

Pete:

Excellent post! I think your point is more about the value of being
open to learning, and its intrinsic value, rather than the value of
stupidity.  If we know our limitations and have a desire to expand
them, there is little that we cannot accomplish!

In 1999 I went into the ER with serious breathing problems.  Long
story short, I had a large benign tumor growing in the right atrium of
my heart.  The heart surgery I had would have put a quintuple bypass
to shame, and I experienced some brain damage (a condition called
'pump head') from being on the bypass for 22 minutes.  For a long time
I had difficulty finding words, finishing thoughts, etc.

At that time I had been a COBOL programmer for 24 years, but had stuck
with the old character based types of programs.  What I decided to do
was learn GUI programming from the ground up.  Again long story short,
I learned all the new techniques and complete rewrote all my modules.
I benefited enormously from this exercise (as did all my users).  And
more importantly, because I exercised it, alot of the damage to my
brain seemed to be mitigated.

At whatever age, learning can be enormously helpful and productive.
Thanks Pete for yet another excellent post!

MattC
```

```
 And he gritted his teeth
```

```
current maintenance cost is pretty good business. It keeps their legacy 
running while they look for an alternative. It buys them 5 years for the 
cost of one year. Five years in IT is a long time and you can do much in 
that time.

Think seriously on what I suggested. If you can show them that moving their 
applications to the Cloud and the Web can extend the life of those 
applications and it won't cost an arm and a leg (by their standards) you 
could have an interesting and quite lucrative contract on your hands.

Whatever you decide, don't undersell your skill.
```

```
OpenCOBOL Features

Features already implemented †
■Part of COBOL 85
■Part of COBOL 2002
■Part of IBM COBOL
■Part of Micro Focus COBOL
■Intrinsic functions
   From 1991 Intrinsic Functions Amendment
■ACOS, ANNUITY, ASIN, ATAN, CHAR, COS, CURRENT-DATE, DATE-OF-INTEGER,
DAY-OF-INTEGER, FACTORIAL, INTEGER, INTEGER-OF-DATE, INTEGER-OF-DAY,
INTEGER-PART, LENGTH, LOG, LOG10, LOWER-CASE, MAX, MEAN, MEDIAN,
MIDRANGE, MIN, MOD, NUMVAL, NUMVAL-C, ORD, ORD-MAX, ORD-MIN, PRESENT-
VALUE, RANDOM, RANGE, REM, REVERSE, SIN, SQRT, STANDARD-DEVIATION,
SUM, TAN, UPPER-CASE, VARIANCE, WHEN-COMPILED,
From the 2002 Standard

■ABS, BYTE-LENGTH, DAY-TO-YYYYDDD, DATE-TO-YYYYMMDD, E, EXCEPTION-
FILE, EXCEPTION-LOCATION, EXCEPTION-STATEMENT, EXCEPTION-STATUS, EXP,
EXP10, FRACTION-PART, LOCALE-DATE, LOCALE-TIME, PI, SIGN, TEST-DATE-
YYYYMMDD, YEAR-TO-YYYY
From draft of next revision:

■SECONDS-FROM-FORMATTED-TIME, SECONDS-PAST-MIDNIGHT, TRIM,
From extensions in other compilers:

■STORED-CHAR-LENGTH,
Unique to OpenCOBOL 1.1 pre-release

■CONCATENATE, SUBSTITUTE, SUBSTITUTE-CASE

Features not implemented
■Intrinsic functions
■BOOLEAN-OF-INTEGER, CHAR-NATIONAL, DAY-TO-YYYYMMM, DISPLAY-OF,
EXCEPTION-FILE-N, EXCEPTION-LOCATION-N, HIGHEST-ALGEBRAIC, INTEGER-OF-
BOOLEAN, LOCALE-COMPARE, LOWEST-ALGEBRAIC, NATIONAL-OF, NUMVAL-F,
STANDARD-COMPARE, TEST-DAY-YYYYDDD,, TEST-NUMVAL, TEST-NUMVAL-C, TEST-
NUMVAL-F
■SCREEN SECTION
Update: SCREEN SECTION is now in OC 1.1 pre-release, and working out
pretty well. ToDo still includes edit by PICTURE

■Embedded SQL

Features won't be implemented
■COMMUNICATION SECTION

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------


No sign of INVOKE.
```

### Math joke

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-20T05:22:41-05:00

```
[From the Instapundit blog]

Math joke from Anna, the bartender and civil engineering student: an 
infinite number of mathematicians walk into a bar. The first one tells the 
bartender he wants a beer. The second one says he wants half a beer. The 
third one says he wants a fourth of a beer.

The bartender puts two beers on the bar and says �You guys need to learn 
your limits.�
```

```
Here in the Washington, DC area there are still those who remember being 
stung by their early UNIVAC purchases; the (perhaps apochryphal) story 
goes that some parts of the 1960 Census were crunched through the Census 
Bureau's machine and when they wanted to compare results in 1970 they 
could find only two machines that were able to read the original media...

... and one of these was in the Smithsonian Institution (also called 
'America's Attic').
```

```
> How about a 9-track tape?

  How about a 7-track tape? 

  That used to be the standard on the first generations of the OS/1100
machines, which used 36-bit words and 6 bit sixths of the word. 

  I still remember the desperate search on comp.sys.unisys from a 
person who had a collection of important scientific data on 7-track 
tape rolls and could not find a machine to read those in... 

  BTW, me too have worked for Sperry/Univac/Unisys for many years. 
Programming COBOL on three different operating systems of Sperry 
Univac, and having written kind of a manual how to program COBOL and 
stay compatible between those systems... 


Cheers,
L.W.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Univac - Remember them?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Charlie <foxgrove@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2011-09-23T12:54:12-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fe41b91b-9cc4-4c69-8225-108c53b9966e@m38g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9drh9tFfsmU1@mid.individual.net> <j5dtpt$171$1@reader1.panix.com> <9dvdopFrbkU2@mid.individual.net> <czd2LKcn8EGd-pn2-ExhY6b13z8BB@lueko.willms.dialin.t-online.de> <jsjm771bevl9jbagptukpfcvki5jurkc62@4ax.com>`

```

> >> How about a 9-track tape?
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Honeywell 115 and 120 had six but characters with native character set
BCD, but no fixed word size.  Each byte in memory had the six data
bits along with a word mark bit (and I think a parity bit).  You used
the word mark to indicate the limit of various instructions.  Happily
we had the COBOL-B compiler and did very little in assembler.  The
compiler ran in 8K without mass storage.  Source on cards in, object
punched onto cards, mag tapes for intermediate storage by the
compiler.  On interesting feature of the compiler was that you only
the first few bytes of identifiers were significant(I think four).
T1A1-ABC matched T1A1-XYZ.  Seven track tape only, usually 556 BPI.
We eventually replaced the H120 computers with a Honeywell 2020

Later worked on DCD Cyber with 60 bit words.  It could use either 6
bit BCD or 8 bit ASCII.  60 Bits was great for floating point, but 8
does not go into 60 evenly.

Later still worked on Honeywell 66 which ran GCOS.  This was an
evolution of the GE mainframes.  I think the word size was 36 bits.
It had hardware emulation of the old Honeywell 2000 series computer.

The COBOL code that was written in the late '60s survived three
generations of mainframes.  Some of it was eventually moved to PC's
where a small bit continues to be used today.

---Charlie
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Univac - Remember them?

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Robin Vowels <robin.vowels@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-09-23T19:51:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<93dc3e01-0290-4948-ab7e-714402a94d67@18g2000yqz.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9drh9tFfsmU1@mid.individual.net> <j5dtpt$171$1@reader1.panix.com> <9dvdopFrbkU2@mid.individual.net> <czd2LKcn8EGd-pn2-ExhY6b13z8BB@lueko.willms.dialin.t-online.de> <jsjm771bevl9jbagptukpfcvki5jurkc62@4ax.com> <fe41b91b-9cc4-4c69-8225-108c53b9966e@m38g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sep 24, 5:54 am, Charlie <foxgr...@shaw.ca> wrote:

> Later worked on DCD Cyber with 60 bit words.  It could use either 6
> bit BCD or 8 bit ASCII.  60 Bits was great for floating point, but 8
> does not go into 60 evenly.

I think that you will find that CDC Cyber dd not use 8-bit ASCII.
Characters were 6 bits.  To get upper and lower-case,
you used a 6-bit escape code, and thus such characters were 12 bits --
giving rise the notation 6/12 bit characters.
```

```
An interesting point, Mr Gunshannon, and it might be considered in light 
of the maxim that 'a difference in quantity makes for a difference in 
quality.  I recall purchasing floppy disk drives with the equivalent of 
cash I found in my sofa-cushions and carrying them in one hand; according 
to http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNIVAC_I :

--begin quoted text:

Originally priced at US$159,000, the UNIVAC I rose in price until they 
were between $1,250,000 and $1,500,000. A total of 46 systems were 
eventually built and delivered.

[snip]

UNIVAC I used 5,200 vacuum tubes,[5] weighed 29,000 pounds (13 metric 
tons), consumed 125 kW, and could perform about 1,905 operations per 
second running on a 2.25 MHz clock. The Central Complex alone (i.e. the 
processor and memory unit) was 4.3 m by 2.4 m by 2.6 m high. The complete 
system occupied more than 35.5 m of floor space.

--end quoted text

>
>Of course, that's when they turn to guys like me.  I can still read all
>of the above.  :-)

Back when I was younger and a bit sharper - imagine such a thing! - I used 
to take a floppy disk out of it's casing and thumb-tack it to a cubicle 
wall after I'd taped a scrap of paper reading 'My Contract' onto it.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: OT: Univac - Remember them?

- **From:** "Herr Willms" <l.willms@domain.invalid>
- **Date:** 2011-09-22T14:00:29+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<czd2LKcn8EGd-pn2-p9JiKelv5XNu@lueko.willms.dialin.t-online.de>`
- **References:** `<9drh9tFfsmU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Am Tue, 20 Sep 2011 13:03:54 UTC,  schrieb "Pete Dashwood" 
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>  auf comp.lang.cobol :

> I came across this and found it very interesting. It even mentions Grace 
> Hopper so it may not be totally off topic...

  and it is wrong to say that Grace Hopper (and her team) alone 
developed COBOL. COBOL was an industry wide effort under direction (or
demand) of the US "defense" department. But when you look at official 
COBOL manuals, you should still find the reference to FLOW-MATIC, 
which was Grace Hopper's work and Univac/Sperry. 

> http://arstechnica.com/tech-policy/news/2011/09/univac-the-troubled-life-of-americas-first-computer.ars
> 
  Missing from this article is also the story of the two lines within 
the Sperry/Univac company, the byte oriented Eckert/Mauchly from 
Philadelphia, and the group in the Twin Cities from where originated 
the later OS/1100 architecture. They worked on a tender from the US 
military for a computer, and gave this the number 13, but this 
expressed in binary: 1101. A successor of this was them 1108, with 
Exec8 as its operating system, and this hardware and software are the 
basis of OS/1100 which was renamed later to OS/2200 (the same number 
of syllabs in English, but in German 11 is only one sylab, but 22 
three...). 


Cheers,
L.W.

-------------------------------------------------------

Falls nicht, ists kein Kaffee, sondern Altï¿½l
```

## Thread: cobol debugger and eXperimental gui cobol — 10 msgs

### cobol debugger and eXperimental gui cobol

- **From:** federico <priolof@libero.it>
- **Date:** 2011-09-25T12:41:33-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e3b16467-4d90-478a-ad3c-d27e820039ab@8g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi all to improve the opensource debugger for opencobol 1.1 i started
also a project to manage a gui by using gtk and opencobol since the
debugger still hasn't got a gui interface..

both project can be visited at www.tp-srl.it

the experimental guicobol is still project but I upload a sample and a
video to show how it is going ahead.


this thread just to ask if there is someone that is using gtk with
cobol (in general) ?


Federico
```
